  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_2d5387a5-c681-4e72-b68d-0b97be2eec4f'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer4652404365910152054"><p>
  <i>I'm not crazy. I don't see what the big deal is about what happened. But apparently someone does think it's a big deal because here I am. I bet it was my mother. She always overreacts.</i>

  <span id="freeText4652404365910152054" style="display:none"><p>
  <i>I'm not crazy. I don't see what the big deal is about what happened. But apparently someone does think it's a big deal because here I am. I bet it was my mother. She always overreacts.</i>
</p><p>Fifteen-year-old Jeff wakes up on New Year's Day to find himself in the hospital. Make that the psychiatric ward. With the nutjobs. Clearly, this is all a huge mistake. Forget about the bandages on his wrists and the notes on his chart. Forget about his problems with his best friend, Allie, and her boyfriend, Burke. Jeff's perfectly fine, perfectly normal, not like the other kids in the hospital with him. Now they've got problems. But a funny thing happens as his forty-five-day sentence drags on: the crazies start to seem less crazy.</p><p>Compelling, witty, and refreshingly real, <i>Suicide Notes</i> is a darkly humorous novel from award-winning author Michael Thomas Ford that examines that fuzzy line between "normal" and the rest of us. </p><p></p></span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/657336-wow-this-seems-super-fucking-albiest">Wow, this seems super fucking albiest. Calling mentally ill people &#39;Crazies&#39; and &#39;Nutjobs&#39;, I shouldn&#39;t even explain what wrong w/ that. The description insists that Jeff&#39;s &quot;perfectly fine, perfectly normal&quot; because as we all know, if you&#39;ve been institutionalized, or are neurodivergent, you&#39;re abnormal, weird, crazy, and totally not &#39;fine&#39;. Way to lose a reader before you even get into the first chapter?</a>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/26653669-john-harrison"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1387214722i/26653669._UY100_CR12,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/26653669-john-harrison">John Harrison</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">God people are moronic. If you're too fragile to make it past the cover, and you spend all your time judging a book before even reading the first page<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">God people are moronic. If you're too fragile to make it past the cover, and you spend all your time judging a book before even reading the first page, maybe you should give up on reading altogether.<br /><br />On another note, this book is far from "ableist" (which, by the way, is the correct spelling of that stupid word). If anything, the biggest theme IN the book is that mental disabilities don't make a person less than. <br /><br />The irony of this comment/complaint is that you've gone and judged the book just as superficially as the main character initially judges his fellow mental patients. What does that say about you, huh?<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=read-2011">read-2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172044942" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer301752906308782226">Believe it or not, this is actually a really funny book. You wouldn't think so based on the title and the subject, but 15-year-old Jeff will have you laughing out loud throughout his story. He's in a mental hospital because he tried to slit his wrists on New Year's Eve, he's surrounded by kids who are clearly crazier than he is, and his doctor (nicknamed "Cat Poop") doesn't seem to understand that there's nothing wrong with him and won't leave him alone. Neither will the various patients who com</span>
  <span id="freeText301752906308782226" style="display:none">Believe it or not, this is actually a really funny book. You wouldn't think so based on the title and the subject, but 15-year-old Jeff will have you laughing out loud throughout his story. He's in a mental hospital because he tried to slit his wrists on New Year's Eve, he's surrounded by kids who are clearly crazier than he is, and his doctor (nicknamed "Cat Poop") doesn't seem to understand that there's nothing wrong with him and won't leave him alone. Neither will the various patients who come and go who keep wanting to fool around with him in the wee hours of the night. <br /><br />All Jeff wants to do is to do his time and to get home--partly because his sister Amanda might call dibs on his vacant room. And he does <i>not</i> want to talk about what happened with his best friend Allie, and how their relationship changed after she started dating her boyfriend Burke.<br /><br />The novel is set up so that each chapter follows a single day in Jeff's 45-day treatment program. As the narrator, Jeff is hilariously dead-pan, self-deprecating, and easy to listen to. He is also kind, curious, confused, and sad beneath the typical teenage guy "I'm fine" attitude, but this takes a little while to come out. What's really interesting about the book being from Jeff's point of view is that the author reveals Jeff's avoidance and self-delusion without our main character really being aware of it, which is a pretty neat trick. And it's all all done with a deft hand and an unerring eye for genuine emotion.<br /><br />I'm still undecided as to whether I should go into detail about what this book is actually about, but I will say that it's pretty important that readers who go into this story are fairly open-minded. In the middle of the drama involving the various patients at the hospital, there are frank discussions about (and depictions of) suicide, abuse, identity, sexuality, and self-loathing that are realistically and honestly portrayed. I did, however, appreciate the author's choice to make Jeff's secret both more complex and less of an extreme situation <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[i.e., it was not abuse in his case that led to his suicide attempt <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>, as I think it's important that we see more stories from this standpoint. The confusion and embarrassment and hurt and fear can sometimes be enough.<br /><br />******************************************************************<br /><br /><i>Excerpts:</i><br /><br />Maybe I can convince my parents to move to France. No one in France cares if you tried to kill yourself. In fact, I think they like you better because you're all tragic.<br /><br />It's not like I've never jacked off. I'm fifteen years old. Of course I do it. Any guy who says he doesn't is lying. That would be like having the coolest video game ever and never playing it. No one's that stupid.<br /><br />******************************************************************<br /><br />The humor and the depth in this exceptionally well-written novel felt incredibly true to life and poignant. I worried about this boy and his denial about himself and I was anxious about whether the people in his life would accept him. We don't get to read stories like this nearly often enough, but they are such an important part of the human experience and I hope we'll see more of them. <br /><br />I also really appreciated the hopeful and optimistic tone that this novel takes, however. It's nice to be reminded not only that there are kids out there who are hurting, but also that there are people out there who care. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="301752906308782226" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_207070237" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207070237" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207070237?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=2006-2010">2006-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=humor-and-satire">humor-and-satire</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207070237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9618799227784180481">This book is an stealthy, <b>cat-like</b> emotional <b>NINJA</b>...<br/><br/>The story started off all <b>whistling</b> and <b>nonchalant</b> like it was going to be a <b>light dose</b> of fluffy teenage angst. Then, halfway through, it <b>crept</b> up behind me, <b>tapped</b> me <b>gently</b> on the <b>feelings</b> and slipped silently into my core to <b>snuggle</b>...Ninja style. <br/><br/>We start off meeting 15 year old Jeff who has just awoken on New Year’s Day after a botched <b>suicide</b> attempt to find himself <b>involuntarily</b> committed to a mental treatment facility for 45 days. Of </span>
  <span id="freeText9618799227784180481" style="display:none">This book is an stealthy, <b>cat-like</b> emotional <b>NINJA</b>...<br /><br />The story started off all <b>whistling</b> and <b>nonchalant</b> like it was going to be a <b>light dose</b> of fluffy teenage angst. Then, halfway through, it <b>crept</b> up behind me, <b>tapped</b> me <b>gently</b> on the <b>feelings</b> and slipped silently into my core to <b>snuggle</b>...Ninja style. <br /><br />We start off meeting 15 year old Jeff who has just awoken on New Year’s Day after a botched <b>suicide</b> attempt to find himself <b>involuntarily</b> committed to a mental treatment facility for 45 days. Of course Jeff knows he’s not crazy. He doesn’t belong with the other “whack jobs.” His suicide attempt was just “something” he did. No reason behind it, he doesn't have problems...and he definitely does NOT want to talk about it. <br /><br />He's your classic untrustworthy narrator. <br /><br />So suicide....mental health facility...teenage drama...all that may have you thinking this book is just a big batch of the blues but that is not the case. Despite the premise, it is surprisingly light in tone and spiced throughout with humor. In fact, up until about the halfway point, I thought that the narrative might have been a tad too flippant and casual to the point where I was actually beginning to become a little disenchanted. <br /><br />Then...<b>KABLOOIE</b>...enter the Ninja!!<br /><br />Turns out the author had just set me up, waited for my guard to drop and then coldcocked me right in the blood pump.<br /><br />Once the true reasons underlying Jeff’s teenage cry for help began to be fleshed and exposed to the light of conscious awareness, the narrative became deeply poignant and I felt a physical ache for Jeff and his all too human unwillingness to confront the root of his disconnect with life. Once the pieces started falling into place and the heart yanking had commenced, I gained a whole new perspective for the book...along with a serious appreciation for it. <br /><br />This story has <b>a lot</b> to say and ended up being far more “important” than I anticipated going in to it. It’s not gorgeous literature and it isn’t the kind of novel you read to be carried away by the lushness of the prose or the clever turns of phrase. However, the writing is good and carries the story well...and the story is what this book is all about. <br /><br />I don’t want to get into specifics of the central core of the narrative because it really snuck up on me and I thought this added to the power of my reaction to it when it occurred. I will just say that this is a book I would recommend both teenagers (despite some very frank language and sexuality) and their parents as I think Ford does an excellent job of realistically portraying teens struggling through life-changing moments.<br /><br />Whether or not you personally relate to Jeff, I think this is a pretty universal story about what it means to be a teen and try to find your place in the cold, chaotic cacophony of the world. <br /><br />I was also very impressed that the author did not stoop to overt sentimentality. The power of the novel owes nothing to gimmicks or any attempts at ham-handed manipulation of the reader’s emotions. No, the strength of the novel lies with the story itself. A story of honest reflections. A story very well told by Mr. Ford. <br /><br />A surprisingly good book. <br /><br />3.5 stars. Highly Recommended. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9618799227784180481" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81565918" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9684100890407615355"><b>Posted at <a href='http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2015/03/suicide-notes.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>Shelf Inflicted</a></b><br/><br/><i>Suicide Notes</i> has 45 chapters, each one representing a day in the life of 15-year-old Jeff, who is in the psychiatric ward of a hospital after his suicide attempt on New Year’s Eve. <br/> <br/>Trust me; this story is not nearly as depressing as it sounds. <br/><br/>Jeff is quick, witty, sarcastic, and absolutely hilarious as he manages to evade any “real” discussion with his psychiatrist, Dr. Katzrupus, also known as Cat Poop, about what made him try to kill himself. <br/><br/>During his 45-day “sent</span>
  <span id="freeText9684100890407615355" style="display:none"><b>Posted at <a href="http://www.shelfinflicted.com/2015/03/suicide-notes.html" rel="nofollow noopener">Shelf Inflicted</a></b><br /><br /><i>Suicide Notes</i> has 45 chapters, each one representing a day in the life of 15-year-old Jeff, who is in the psychiatric ward of a hospital after his suicide attempt on New Year’s Eve. <br /> <br />Trust me; this story is not nearly as depressing as it sounds. <br /><br />Jeff is quick, witty, sarcastic, and absolutely hilarious as he manages to evade any “real” discussion with his psychiatrist, Dr. Katzrupus, also known as Cat Poop, about what made him try to kill himself. <br /><br />During his 45-day “sentence”, Jeff learns more about himself as he endures individual and group therapy, makes friends, experiences grief and loss, and comes to terms with his sexuality.<br /><br />The story takes a more serious turn when Jeff gradually opens up to Cat Poop, and details of his family life, his friendships, the events that led to his suicide attempt, and the reason for it begin to unfold. <br /><br />I really loved this story told from Jeff’s perspective and had a very difficult time putting it down. His thoughts, feelings, confusion and pain all rang true and brought me back to my own teenage years. I would highly recommend this story to teens that are gay, straight, or somewhere in between, and to adults who remember what it was like, or just want to understand. <br /></span>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review81565918">

<div id="review_1293809368" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1293809368" />
    <a title="✦❋Arianna✦❋" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23370948-arianna"><img alt="✦❋Arianna✦❋" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420671997p2/23370948.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1293809368?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="✦❋Arianna✦❋" class="user" itemprop="url" name="✦❋Arianna✦❋" href="/user/show/23370948-arianna">✦❋Arianna✦❋</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1293809368" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7121563250928333923"><b>4 Stars!!</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"That sounds so weird: kill yourself. It makes it sound like you tried to murder someone, only that someone is you. But killing someone is wrong and I don't think suicide is. It's my life, right?"</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/><br/><i>“Suicide Notes”</i> it was like nothing I’ve expected. Despite the title and despite the blurb this was a quite entertaining read. It’s a YA novel/coming of age/realistic fiction that was interesting and refreshing and hold my interest not only with its plot, but with its main character</span>
  <span id="freeText7121563250928333923" style="display:none"><b>4 Stars!!</b><br /><br /><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i>"That sounds so weird: kill yourself. It makes it sound like you tried to murder someone, only that someone is you. But killing someone is wrong and I don't think suicide is. It's my life, right?"</i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br /><br /><i>“Suicide Notes”</i> it was like nothing I’ve expected. Despite the title and despite the blurb this was a quite entertaining read. It’s a YA novel/coming of age/realistic fiction that was interesting and refreshing and hold my interest not only with its plot, but with its main character as well. <br /><br />The story is about Jeff a 15 years old boy, who after he tried to slit his wrists on New Year’s Eve he’s committed by his parents in a hospital. He has to spend 45 days in a psychiatric ward and he’s not happy at all. After all he’s not crazy. At least not like the other kids there, who seem to be nuts. He has to participate in individual counselling sessions with Dr. Katzrupus (whom he nicknames Dr. Cat Poop) and in group counselling with some of the other patients. Jeff is not depressed. He just only to forget about ‘this incident’ and go home. While he tells everyone that he doesn’t have any reason for what he did, everyone there wants to talk about it.<br /><br />This novel is not only about suicide. It’s also about family dysfunctions, abuse, accepting who you are and accepting your sexuality. It has 45 chapters, every chapter representing a day in Jeff’s new program. I really liked how the author structured the book. It goes without saying there’s a reason for it. While the first half of the story is pretty light for a book with a premise like this one and even funny at times, the second part is more ‘serious’, emotional and intense. I really liked how balanced the story was. <i>“Suicide Notes”</i> was a very refreshing read and pretty addicting. I really had to know more about Jeff and why did he want to commit suicide.<br /> <br />In the first half we met the other patients in the psychiatric ward. Their voices are different, unique and like Jeff’s felt real. In the second half we get to know Jeff better, his relationship with his sister (I loved them together and their banter) and his parents, his relationship with his best friend Allie and why he wanted to commit suicide. <br /><br />Jeff was a very vivid character. His voice was so honest and his growth as character is fantastically done. I loved his characterization. From the beginning he’s very easy to like. He’s funny, witty and IMO mature for his age. He can be sweet and gentle, thoughtful and compassionate. At first he’s confused, then angry, but as the story progresses he realizes he can’t go home. At least not yet. I have to say I loved his sarcasm and his cynicism. His character felt real and I’m sure it will feel relatable for some readers. There were moments when I LOL with Jeff, but there were also moments I just wanted to give him a hug. I really felt for him at times and I was really happy for when he started realizing that it’s ok to be different.<br /> <br />If you’re looking for a YA novel that deals with important sensitive topics, written in 1st POV from a male’s perspective I really recommend this one!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7121563250928333923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_150336586" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150336586" />
    <a title="Flannery" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/652877-flannery"><img alt="Flannery" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312843258p2/652877.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150336586?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Flannery" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Flannery" href="/user/show/652877-flannery">Flannery</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/652877-flannery?shelf=reviewed-2011">reviewed-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/652877-flannery?shelf=wishlist-for-dtbs-to-keep">wishlist-for-dtbs-to-keep</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/652877-flannery?shelf=kcls">kcls</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/652877-flannery?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/652877-flannery?shelf=young-adult-teen">young-adult-teen</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150336586" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11859920011248655984">It feels a little weird to say that I felt a book about a 45-day program in a juvenile psychiatric unit was really funny. But it was—in parts. This book, written in journal entries from day one of the program until the last day, focuses on Jeff’s evaluation of why he tried to kill himself. His voice is reminiscent of Holden Caulfield, only he doesn’t call everyone phonies—just whackjobs. <br/><br/>Jeff introduces us to the other young adults in the unit, some of whom come and go during his stay. He also h</span>
  <span id="freeText11859920011248655984" style="display:none">It feels a little weird to say that I felt a book about a 45-day program in a juvenile psychiatric unit was really funny. But it was—in parts. This book, written in journal entries from day one of the program until the last day, focuses on Jeff’s evaluation of why he tried to kill himself. His voice is reminiscent of Holden Caulfield, only he doesn’t call everyone phonies—just whackjobs. <br /><br />Jeff introduces us to the other young adults in the unit, some of whom come and go during his stay. He also has to see a psychiatrist during his time in the program, the delightful Dr. Katzrupus. (or Cat Poop, as Jeff dubs him) At first, I felt like we weren’t getting to know each supporting character well enough but isn’t that the point? I mean, Jeff is in this program solely to figure out what <b>his</b> issues are. These are <b>his</b> journal entries we are reading. And it all felt real—I felt anxious with him, sad for him, mortified with him, and so hopeful that maybe it would all work out. The relationship he had with his sister made me laugh the most, though. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[And the way he came out to his family was just perfect. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />While this book definitely deals with a lot of morbid topics, the feel is decidedly optimistic for the most part. I enjoyed the fact that Jeff was very matter-of-fact about most things and the conversations he had with people didn’t really tiptoe around the serious stuff. His doctor/patient relationship with Dr. Katzrupus was a highlight as well.<br /><br />I’d definitely recommend this book to anyone looking for a male voice in the female-saturated YA world. Though I hope this wouldn’t affect anyone’s choice to read a book or not, there are some M/M sexual scenes. Just putting that out there. I’ll definitely read more from this author.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11859920011248655984" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_81503121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81503121" />
    <a title="Thomas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas"><img alt="Thomas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1567953607p2/2018505.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81503121?book_show_action=true">Dec 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Thomas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Thomas" href="/user/show/2018505-thomas">Thomas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=five-stars">five-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=lgbtq">lgbtq</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2018505-thomas?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81503121" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8785296558583932163">Jeff isn't crazy. He isn't like all the nut jobs in the psych ward he finds himself in. He doesn't use drugs to get high, and he doesn't have an eating disorder that makes him throw up his latest meal every ten seconds. However, he does have bandages covering his wrists like a mummy and he does recall getting into an argument with his best friend Allie, and he's pretty sure it had something to do with her boyfriend Burke. While Jeff spends the next forty-five days in the hospital under a special</span>
  <span id="freeText8785296558583932163" style="display:none">Jeff isn't crazy. He isn't like all the nut jobs in the psych ward he finds himself in. He doesn't use drugs to get high, and he doesn't have an eating disorder that makes him throw up his latest meal every ten seconds. However, he does have bandages covering his wrists like a mummy and he does recall getting into an argument with his best friend Allie, and he's pretty sure it had something to do with her boyfriend Burke. While Jeff spends the next forty-five days in the hospital under a special care program, he slowly starts to meet new people and realize why he's there in the first place.<br /><br />Finally, a book worth of comparison to <i>What They Always Tell Us</i>. The two books are completely different, but they gave me that special feeling you get sometimes when you read a really great book, like "why do I spend all this time reading subpar books when I could be reading books like this?" That's the feeling I got when I read <i>Suicide Notes</i>.<br /><br />Even though the title and dust jacket are a bit dry and seem to be a typical YA fare <i>Suicide Notes</i> is completely different. It's edgy, honest, real, and provocative. Jeff is snide, sarcastic, and funny as a whip. I laughed out loud various times in the book due to really hilarious scenes and also because Jeff mentions a wide variety of objects and ideas that really come out of nowhere. What makes this even better is that Ford kept me laughing even though the entire plot itself was supposed to be depressing. He writes it well enough to keep me on the edge of my seat and wanting more.<br /><br />Actually, it only took me about three-four hours to read <i>Suicide Notes</i>. The small pages might have contributed to that a little bit, but I think it's because the book is just compulsively readable. I seriously could not put it down, even when I felt my treadmill calling my name. I just had to know what happens to Jeff, what he's going through, the next big event. The plot speed was just right - it moves fast, but nothing is rushed.<br /><br />One more thing great about this book is that Jeff was an awesome character, inside and out. Although you might figure out the big "secret" ahead of time, the other plot devices kept me occupied and yearning for more. Jeff never became out of character for no reason, and just the emotional framework of his perspective really drew me in. I would highly recommend this book to basically everyone, but there are just a few scenes I know older adults or really strict librarians would frown upon (even though they don't take awhile from the book at all).</span>
  <a data-text-id="8785296558583932163" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/295820477" />
    <a title="Richard Derus" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus"><img alt="Richard Derus" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494992210p2/3920693.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/295820477?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Derus" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Derus" href="/user/show/3920693-richard-derus">Richard Derus</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer295820477" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10525550418728135970">Real Rating: 2.5* of five<br/><br/>All the points are for the ending, which is entirely worth the long, tedious, acne-inducing slog to get there.<br/><br/>Seriously...does the world NEED to hear about adolescence anymore? Is there something we missed, as adults, while going through that training ground for evil demons called "junior high" (that's middle school for the under-fifty set)? If so, is it something that we actually *need*?<br/><br/>Basically...no more. No no no. Poke me with a fork, I'm done.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_207268928" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/207268928" />
    <a title="Limonessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4147373-limonessa"><img alt="Limonessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424025988p2/4147373.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/207268928?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Limonessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Limonessa" href="/user/show/4147373-limonessa">Limonessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780062043078" href="/book/show/9952477-suicide-notes">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Limonessa by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4551536-maja-the-nocturnal-library">Maja (The Nocturnal Library)</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4147373-limonessa?shelf=ya-lit">ya-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4147373-limonessa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer207268928" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14554358244089235995">What struck me most about this book - and unsettled me, to be honest - is the brutality of it, sugarcoated by Jeff's self-deprecating irony, witticism and sarcastic outlook on adolescence. He is one of those characters I particularly appreciate in teen lit for their no-nonsense attitude, for just telling things how they are. An honest, non-emo voice. <br/><br/>The themes approached in this book are not light, despite seemingly narrated in a light-hearted way: teen suicide, familial dysfunctions, personal </span>
  <span id="freeText14554358244089235995" style="display:none">What struck me most about this book - and unsettled me, to be honest - is the brutality of it, sugarcoated by Jeff's self-deprecating irony, witticism and sarcastic outlook on adolescence. He is one of those characters I particularly appreciate in teen lit for their no-nonsense attitude, for just telling things how they are. An honest, non-emo voice. <br /><br />The themes approached in this book are not light, despite seemingly narrated in a light-hearted way: teen suicide, familial dysfunctions, personal identity. The story starts with Jeff waking up in the psych ward of an hospital, after having attempted suicide. He's supposed to spend 6 weeks being treated there and to understand the reasons why he hurt himself. <br />Despite being told in 1st person POV, Jeff is in self-denial and does not want to acknowledge the origin of his problems or what really happened that led to him taking such a definitive and desperate action. So we, the readers, are completely left in the dark about pretty much everything that took place before him being hospitalized.<br />But slowly, as Jeff gradually comes around and faces the bitter consequences of what he's done, we discover bits and pieces of the puzzle that eventually will give him, and consequently us, realization of his real problem. I know this sounds really vague but it's better to discover Jeff's motives by reading this book. I really liked this narrative strategy, it spurs the reader to go on keeping the interest high and makes the discoveries all the more dramatic. <br /><br />Aside from the heavy theme of the book, be warned that there are some sex scenes which put this book in the more adult section of the YA genre. Pretty graphic and raw, too. Yet, I wish this book were read by all teens and I hope by the time my kids will grow up I will still remember this book, so that I can give it to them to read. <br />Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14554358244089235995" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1105062244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17094813226846901194"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1416490504i/11976692.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i><br/>“If you ever manage to become perfect, you have to die instantly before you ruin things for everyone else.” </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br/><br/><br/>It was my third book by Michael Thomas Ford and he didn't disappoint me. The first one, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10324874.The_Road_Home' title='The Road Home by Michael Thomas Ford' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Road Home</a>, a contemporary romance about <i>coming home</i> with a nice unexpected twist of mystery in it, has sparked my interest for his works. The second one, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/98081.Full_Circle' title='Full Circle by Michael Thomas Ford' rel='nofollow noopener'>Full Circle</a>, an amazing epic story of love, friendship and male bonding, touched me greatly and made me feel and think about even days</span>
  <span id="freeText17094813226846901194" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1416490504i/11976692.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><blockquote>
  <b>
    <i><br>“If you ever manage to become perfect, you have to die instantly before you ruin things for everyone else.” </i>
  </b>
</blockquote><br><br><br>It was my third book by Michael Thomas Ford and he didn't disappoint me. The first one, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10324874.The_Road_Home" title="The Road Home by Michael Thomas Ford" rel="nofollow noopener">The Road Home</a>, a contemporary romance about <i>coming home</i> with a nice unexpected twist of mystery in it, has sparked my interest for his works. The second one, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/98081.Full_Circle" title="Full Circle by Michael Thomas Ford" rel="nofollow noopener">Full Circle</a>, an amazing epic story of love, friendship and male bonding, touched me greatly and made me feel and think about even days after I'd finished it. Probably my favourite of this author for now and for sure one of my favourite books of this year. <br><br>And now <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3097601.Suicide_Notes" title="Suicide Notes by Michael Thomas Ford" rel="nofollow noopener">Suicide Notes</a>, a YA novella. Totally different story. And you know what? <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/56370.Michael_Thomas_Ford" title="Michael Thomas Ford" rel="nofollow noopener">Michael Thomas Ford</a> wrote it with a such skilful feeling of lightness and freshness as if he has never written nothing else in his life than YA books. <br><br><br>The title, SUICIDE NOTES, immediately reminded me of the epistolary novels of Franz Kafka and Fyodor Dostoyevsky. And I would have probably skipped it if my gaze hadn't incidentally caught the genre that 34 readers(at least it was so many at that moment) defined this book into. <b>Humor</b>. What?! Me: Is it supposed to be funny?! <br><br>Now I know-YES. IT IS FUNNY. But not only.<br><br>Don't MISINTERPRET the title!<br><br><br>I have always believed that some serious things are more impressive if they are told not with an iron face. And I'm glad that the author chosen this form of telling. I LOVE the characters that have a sense of humour, I adore the authors who can skilfully use their unique sense of humour as an ingenious tool in their books. Michael Thomas Ford is one of them.<br><br>Fifteen-year-old Jeff wakes up on New Year's Day to find himself in the hospital. After he tried to commit suicide. So it is not a story that ends with a suicide. The story begins AFTER he successfully FAILED to commit suicide. (OMG, can I say it this way?)<br>He HAS to spend 45 days in the psychiatric ward. With another patients. But Jeff is not crazy. The other-yes, they're nuts, but not Jeff. All he wants is to get out of there and forget this <i>incident. </i>He is not going to do this stuff like a group therapy, these silly psychotherapeutic sessions and answering the stupid questions! But he can drive his doc up the wall and force him to threw him out. He is here by mistake, it is as clear as day. <br>It is what he believes. But is it really so? And WHY did he cut his wrists? <br><br>45 days of the Jeff's life(I have to say here-his POV is hilarious, sarcastic, thoughtful and simply brilliant!), 45 chapters of pure reading enjoyment(believe me, this book is better than 10 blockbusters together!), 295 pages that won't leave your indifferent(if you have your heart on the right side, of course.)<br><br><b>I laughed LAUD, I cry quietly, I finished it with a broad smile on my face.</b> <br><br>I see this book not only as a very entertaining read, it is a tiny but significant contribution to a very serious topic. I've read some reviews, and I can't understand the statements like "nobody has to kill himself because of being gay in our modern time." Right. We've achieved a lot in this direction, we made a big progress towards considering GAY people as a normal phenomenon of our everyday life (DID WE?). <br><br>The truth is - it's easy to talk how easy it is to be smart if you're 40 years old. Jeff is 15. And in spite of his big mouth, he is just an insecure teenager who feels and knows that he is different, but who doesn't have enough life experience and guts to make the right conclusions and decision to handle perfectly all those different and painful situations in his teeny life.<br><br>We have to make our own experiences, bad and good, the ones that will make us deliciously melting when we're 80(if it is possible at all) and the the ones that could be the only positive points on our "+ and - of Alzheimer's disease"-list. <br>Besides this book is not about a sudden realizing of being gay.<br><br>Anyway, it was not the main reason for WHY Jeff did IT to himself. <br><br>But I'm talking too much. Ignore me.<br>Just read this book.<br> <br>And make me happy loving it.<br><br>Please, love it.<br><br>Thank you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17094813226846901194" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText15006990884369864523" style="display:none">Y'all ever read a book and then regret reading it? Not that you hate it but you just can't stop thinking about it and you can relate it, can feel all the emotions the characters went through. This is that kinda book. I wanted to hug them all!!<br />I hope everyone struggling out there find help and way outta their sufferings.<br /><br />Here are some of my favorite excerpts:<br /><br /><i>“No one ever tells you that when your heart breaks, you can feel it. You stop breathing, and for a while you can’t remember how. When you finally do, it feels like your throat has closed up, like you’re trying to suck air through a straw.”</i><br /><br /><i>“I really do think I was flying around in space, though. At least for a little while. I remember thinking that I’d finally find out whether anyone lives on Mars or not. Then it was like someone grabbed me by the foot and yanked me down, back toward Earth. <div id="review_1086008515" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1086008515" />
    <a title="C Steiner" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28214497-c-steiner"><img alt="C Steiner" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408450484p2/28214497.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1086008515?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="C Steiner" class="user" itemprop="url" name="C Steiner" href="/user/show/28214497-c-steiner">C Steiner</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1086008515&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1086008515" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7788139236874559973">The saving grace of this book for me - what brought it up from one star to two - is that I needed to keep reading it in hopes that the writing would suddenly interest me. Books about mentally ill teens are rare enough, and books about LGBT youth are even scarcer. However, <u>Suicide Notes</u> was overall a disappointment on both fronts.<br/><br/>The book opens with 15-year-old Jeff arriving in a mental hospital on New Year's Day after a suicide attempt, reluctant to serve his 45-day "sentence". From the get-go, </span>
  <span id="freeText7788139236874559973" style="display:none">The saving grace of this book for me - what brought it up from one star to two - is that I needed to keep reading it in hopes that the writing would suddenly interest me. Books about mentally ill teens are rare enough, and books about LGBT youth are even scarcer. However, <u>Suicide Notes</u> was overall a disappointment on both fronts.<br /><br />The book opens with 15-year-old Jeff arriving in a mental hospital on New Year's Day after a suicide attempt, reluctant to serve his 45-day "sentence". From the get-go, his voice is too immature to pass for 15. After nicknaming his psychiatrist Dr. Katzrupus "Cat Poop", he continues to giggle about it for the remaining 44 days. He gives similar nicknames to all the nurses on the ward and refuses to any therapeutic exercises the least bit seriously. As someone who has been through a similar program, albeit at a younger age, this was extraordinarily irritating to read, given the multitude of people who would like help like this but cannot receive it. <br /><br />Throughout the novel, Jeff repetitively finds himself in sexually compromising situations with two other patients, a pixie-ish girl named Sadie and a football player named Rankin. Already, this is unrealistic, since they are in a four or five person psych ward and should be checked on far more often than would permit these encounters. In addition, these two people carry different tones for Jeff's narration. When Jeff is with Sadie, he describes the situations as more comfortable and reciprocated. He at least seems to want to be with Sadie, until he attempts to have sex with her and can't bring himself to do so. This throws him off and makes him more upset than necessary.<br /><br />In contrast, his times with Rankin border on sexual assault. He is never the one to initiate a situation with Rankin, with the other boy climbing into his bed without permission or leading him into the showers and forcing him to perform oral sex. Jeff seems uncomfortable with all of these situations, and while he acts curious to find out, for example, what a penis looks like up close, he is disconnected when narrating these encounters. Afterwards, he always talks about how disgusting he feels, or how he wishes it had been a dream.<br /><br />Some day in the late 30s, we find out that the reason that Jeff attempted suicide is because he realized he was gay when developing and then acting on a crush on his best friend's boyfriend. This caused her to break off her friendship with him, and due to his negative attitude towards queer men in general, start to hate himself and deny who he was. When he finally admits that he is gay, he justifies it with his encounters with Sadie and Rankin. He says that since he was unable to have sex with Sadie, it showed that girls were not for him, and that his sexual encounters with Rankin felt "right", despite the sickly feelings that accompanied them. At this point, I had to put the book down and think about what was happening. Somehow, hardly consensual sexual encounters felt "right"? Regardless of whether or not Jeff was gay, this is not the right message for any book to send.<br /><br />My final problem with this was how Jeff reacted to Sadie's suicide at the end of the book. He is momentarily shocked, but then mentions it for another four pages total at best in the last few days of the book. His sexuality somehow overshadowed the fact that his closest friend, in the ward and probably in the world at that point, killed herself out of nowhere. Sadie seemed like she would have remained a part of his life once they had left the ward, and he barely thinks of her once she is gone, a highly unrealistic reaction.<br /><br />Overall, I would not recommend this book unless a reader was extremely desperate for books about mental illness and/or LGBT youth. It read like a poor attempt at replicating Ned Vizzini's <u>It's Kind Of A Funny Story</u>, and was not worth the time I spent on it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7788139236874559973" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_575300862" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/575300862" />
    <a title="Natalie Monroe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6688207-natalie-monroe"><img alt="Natalie Monroe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339730817p2/6688207.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/575300862?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie Monroe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie Monroe" href="/user/show/6688207-natalie-monroe">Natalie Monroe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6688207-natalie-monroe?shelf=lazy-open-endings">lazy-open-endings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6688207-natalie-monroe?shelf=real-problems">real-problems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6688207-natalie-monroe?shelf=pleasantly-surprised">pleasantly-surprised</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6688207-natalie-monroe?shelf=laugh-out-loud-humor">laugh-out-loud-humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6688207-natalie-monroe?shelf=lgbtqia-if-you-squint">lgbtqia-if-you-squint</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6688207-natalie-monroe?shelf=bubbling-under-5-stars">bubbling-under-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6688207-natalie-monroe?shelf=mental-illness">mental-illness</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer575300862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11011098475176702654"><b>4.5 stars</b><br/><br/><blockquote>
  <i>"That sounds so weird: kill yourself. It makes it sound like you tried to murder someone, only that someone is you. But killing someone is wrong and I don't think suicide is. It's my life, right?"<br/></i>
</blockquote><br/><br/>There was a period in my life when all I read were issue-driven contemporaries. Eating disorders, suicides, rape... the works. But then all that excess of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7128.Jodi_Picoult' title='Jodi Picoult' rel='nofollow noopener'>Jodi Picoult</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10003.Laurie_Halse_Anderson' title='Laurie Halse Anderson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Laurie Halse Anderson</a> made me seriously depressed and I moved on to the bestselling Paranormal Romances at the time. Bo</span>
  <span id="freeText11011098475176702654" style="display:none"><b>4.5 stars</b><br><br><blockquote>
  <i>"That sounds so weird: kill yourself. It makes it sound like you tried to murder someone, only that someone is you. But killing someone is wrong and I don't think suicide is. It's my life, right?"<br></i>
</blockquote><br><br>There was a period in my life when all I read were issue-driven contemporaries. Eating disorders, suicides, rape... the works. But then all that excess of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7128.Jodi_Picoult" title="Jodi Picoult" rel="nofollow noopener">Jodi Picoult</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/10003.Laurie_Halse_Anderson" title="Laurie Halse Anderson" rel="nofollow noopener">Laurie Halse Anderson</a> made me seriously depressed and I moved on to the bestselling Paranormal Romances at the time. Boy, was that a whole different bucket of depression.<br><br>Let's hear it for bad life choices.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1414552742ra/11666693.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Suicide Notes is unlike any YA issue-driven contemporary I have ever read. For one, it's <b>funny</b>.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>""We need to run a few tests," he said <br><br>[...]<br><br>"I'm not so good at tests," I said instead. "Especially pop quizzes. Could I maybe have some study time first? I wouldn't want to bring the curve down for the whole class or anything."<br></i>
</blockquote><br><br>I laughed more while reading this book than Tina Fey's autobiography, which, by the way, isn't all that funny. <b>The MC, Jeff, is delightfully snarky.</b> Usually for these kinds of books, the main character's thoughts are scattered and disorientated and often, you feel as though you're sloughing through of a swamp of purple metaphors. Or they're so filled of self-deprecating hatred, you cringe. It's written in purple prose too, by the way. It seems to be a general rule.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1414552742i/11666694._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Jeff isn't like that. He basically <b>blows all the preconceptions and stereotypes of suicidal people out of the water.</b> He doesn't write sad poetry or listen to death metal. He wasn't bullied regularly and has good relationships with his family. He's simply your average sarcastic teenage boy (can I get a what-what for Percy Jackson?), who happens to be suicidal. Then one day, something happened and he broke. Parents will freak about this, but sometimes it just happens without warning. You can insure against a car crash and a fire, but you can't insure against life. <br><br>The <b>writing is simple, but effective.</b> It actually feels like the narrator is talking to you and telling you the story, and it's a writing style I appreciate. At the same time, it contains snippets of the sort of <b>unexpected metaphors</b> you'd find in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19063.The_Book_Thief" title="The Book Thief by Markus Zusak" rel="nofollow noopener">The Book Thief</a>.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>“And anyway, the truth isn't all that great. I mean, what's the truth? Planes falling out of the sky. Buses blowing up and ripping little kids into millions of pieces. Twelve-year-olds raping people and then shooting them in the head so they can't tell. I can't watch the news anymore or look at the papers. It's like whoever sits up there in Heaven has this big bag of really crappy stuff, and once or twice a day she or he reaches in and sprinkles a little bit of it over the world and makes everything crazy, like fairy dust that's past its expiration date.” <br><br>“One time Allie and I skipped school and went to see this foreign film called Los Diablos, where these villagers found a glowing blue ball and peeled pieces off of it to see what was inside. Only the ball was really radioactive, and they all died from the poison. I think that’s what happens when you look too deep inside for the truth. The poison comes out, and you die, even though you have beautiful glowing pieces of blue truth in your fingers.” </i>
</blockquote><br><br>I like metaphors, but believe they should be used sparingly.<br><br>The mystery of <b>why Jeff decided to kill himself is handled very well.</b> It's not like in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6186357.The_Maze_Runner__Maze_Runner___1_" title="The Maze Runner (Maze Runner, #1) by James Dashner" rel="nofollow noopener">The Maze Runner</a>, where they dangle the truth tantalizingly in front of your face, but never let you know. It's done right, with foreshadowing and hints and you can piece it together along the way. I admit I "Yes!"ed when I found out my deduction was correct.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1409824372ra/11027252.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The<b> ending left too many loose threads</b> for my taste though. There were so many questions left unanswered: What happened to Rankin? Why was Bone in the ward? What will become of Martha? Will Jeff <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[make up with Allie? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> Should I have spaghetti or McDonald's for lunch tomorrow? <br><br>Sorry, got distracted. I typing this before lunch and there's chocolate cake in the fridge.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1414552843ra/11666699.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Bottom line: Suicide Notes is amazing and unconventional. Now go read it while I raid the kitchen. Table manners optional.</["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></["br"]></span>
  <a data-text-id="11011098475176702654" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_510040674" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/510040674" />
    <a title="Irina Elena" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7664770-irina-elena"><img alt="Irina Elena" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587303756p2/7664770.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/510040674?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Irina Elena" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Irina Elena" href="/user/show/7664770-irina-elena">Irina Elena</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780062043078" href="/book/show/9952477-suicide-notes">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7664770-irina-elena?shelf=read-in-2014">read-in-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7664770-irina-elena?shelf=ya-realistic">ya-realistic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7664770-irina-elena?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7664770-irina-elena?shelf=disabilities-disorders-and-diseases">disabilities-disorders-and-diseases</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7664770-irina-elena?shelf=4-star">4-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer510040674" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7133665903861541347">I used to think this was sort of a contemporary YA cult, for some reason, but I just realised that not many of my friends have read it. In any case, it <i>should</i> be a cult, if nothing else because it's a book that can help. It can help teens understand themselves and others and it can help adults understand teens.<br/><br/>It's divided in 45 chapters, one for each day Jeff spends in the psychiatric ward of a hospital following his failed (duh) suicide attempt, but it feels like 45 minutes. I gobbled it up in</span>
  <span id="freeText7133665903861541347" style="display:none">I used to think this was sort of a contemporary YA cult, for some reason, but I just realised that not many of my friends have read it. In any case, it <i>should</i> be a cult, if nothing else because it's a book that can help. It can help teens understand themselves and others and it can help adults understand teens.<br /><br />It's divided in 45 chapters, one for each day Jeff spends in the psychiatric ward of a hospital following his failed (duh) suicide attempt, but it feels like 45 minutes. I gobbled it up in less than 24 hours - it had me captivated, horrified, saddened, amused and absolutely enthralled.<br /><br />Jeff is a weirdly lovable protagonist. Thinking in stereotypes, he is the epitome of a fifteen-year-old with his cynicism, sarcasm and entertaining fuck-all attitude, but there is a sweetness and gentleness to him that (get ready - this is going to sound really lame) only comes out for the worthy, the people he considers more deserving of kindness because of who they are and what they've been through. That may sound arbitrary and incongruous, but it actually shows a part of him, buried under all the teenage attitude, that cares deeply about others, a protective streak that's going to make him a wonderful person in the years to come.<br /><br />His voice reminded me a bit of Greg Gaines's, from Jesse Andrews's <i>Me and Earl and the Dying Girl</i>, only it's much more <s>bearable</s> likeable, because his story is rigidly structured and chronologically narrated, and the flashbacks are delivered rarely and on a strictly need-to-know basis.<br />Jeff has this strange ability to come up with wonderful concepts and imagery and deliver them off-handedly, as if unaware of their beauty - but MTF is perfectly well aware of what he's created: naked skeleton trees juggling cotton balls and snowflakes like galaxies rushing by are the sort of similes you don't come across everyday, and they strike that much more powerfully because of that.<br /><br />He is unaware of his own feelings for the most part, and those he's aware of he tends to hide from himself and the reader, but he makes some remarkable points all throughout the book, like this one, which I felt the need to include because it's probably something we've all thought about once or twice, and if we haven't, we should.<br />[I'll spoiler-tag it because it reveals a major plot point - but it's a fairly obvious one, if nothing else because of the GR tags, so you can just go ahead and click it imo.]<br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  <blockquote>
    <i>So now I’m thinking about it. I’m imagining sitting down with my parents and actually saying, “I’m gay.” And you know what? It makes me a little mad. I mean, straight guys don’t have to sit their parents down and tell them they like girls. Everyone just assumes that they do. But if you’re gay, everybody makes this ginormous deal out of it. You practically have to hold a news conference and take out an ad in the newspaper. Why? Just because it’s not what most people do? That doesn’t seem fair.<br />[...] Why is it that you have to warn people about who you are? Why can’t it just be something that happens?</i>
  </blockquote>
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />This one is a bit disjointed and awkwardly put together as far as reviews go, but I'm tired and in the mood for the sort of abstract thoughtfulness that involves staring at the ceiling and listening to music (that could be easily confused with laziness, but do not let youself be misled), so I'll leave you with this: it's a clever, endearing, difficult little gem, transparent but multifaceted, that will make you smile and think and possibly, if you're the mushy type, shed a tear or two - and it's all worth it.<br />Very warmly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7133665903861541347" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35646317" />
    <a title="Rachael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1264852-rachael"><img alt="Rachael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1214345104p2/1264852.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35646317?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachael" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachael" href="/user/show/1264852-rachael">Rachael</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1264852-rachael?shelf=so-very-very-gay">so-very-very-gay</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1264852-rachael?shelf=kids-books">kids-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1264852-rachael?shelf=suicide">suicide</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35646317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1086459832786669811">I'll say it flat out: I detested this book. If I had read it ten or fifteen years ago, I may have thought it was okay, but this isn't the early nineties anymore. Gay kids don't always have to hate themselves and try to commit suicide. Even if it does actually happen sometimes in real life, isn't it better to STOP writing books about how troubled and messed up gay kids are, and focus instead on writing books about how gay kids lead, I don't know, happy and productive lives? Think Alex Sanchez, Da</span>
  <span id="freeText1086459832786669811" style="display:none">I'll say it flat out: I detested this book. If I had read it ten or fifteen years ago, I may have thought it was okay, but this isn't the early nineties anymore. Gay kids don't always have to hate themselves and try to commit suicide. Even if it does actually happen sometimes in real life, isn't it better to STOP writing books about how troubled and messed up gay kids are, and focus instead on writing books about how gay kids lead, I don't know, happy and productive lives? Think Alex Sanchez, David Levithan, Brian Sloan, James Howe, etc. All of these authors write fairly realistic portrayals of gay teens without drawing on the old 'being gay makes you want to kill yourself' stereotype.<br />Add to this fundamental problem of an outdated storyline some fairly mundane, if not downright weak, writing, a main character who is not particularly interesting (generic teenage standoffish behavior seems to be his only real personality trait), and a 'mystery' that was obvious within the first couple of chapters, and you come up with a book that is pretty lackluster.<br />And then it gets downright offensive. I don't know about you, but when if I were to wake up to a naked person in my bed touching me sexually, and I said 'don't' . . . that sounds like assault. How can an author write a book for teens that includes this type of behavior without making it a point to state that it is basically rape? It's downright irresponsible. <br />Having read some of Ford's adult novels, I know that he has talent as a writer. However, being able to write well for adults doesn't always translate into writing well for children or teens. In this case, he failed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1086459832786669811" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1922497562" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1922497562" />
    <a title="Pavellit" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54586399-pavellit"><img alt="Pavellit" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1482125863p2/54586399.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1922497562?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pavellit" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pavellit" href="/user/show/54586399-pavellit">Pavellit</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54586399-pavellit?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54586399-pavellit?shelf=ya-na">ya-na</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1922497562" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2592133778144383298">The time when a prominent YA gay character matures emotionally, regardless of using an idealized MM romance with sexy edges approach or just a window into a gay realistic man’s world, is and it's gonna be so touching subject to me. Until gay guys don’t have to sit their parents down and tell them they like boys without making this ginormous deal out of it.<i>'You practically have to hold a news conference and take out an ad in the newspaper. Why? Why is it that you have to warn people about who you</i></span>
  <span id="freeText2592133778144383298" style="display:none">The time when a prominent YA gay character matures emotionally, regardless of using an idealized MM romance with sexy edges approach or just a window into a gay realistic man’s world, is and it's gonna be so touching subject to me. Until gay guys don’t have to sit their parents down and tell them they like boys without making this ginormous deal out of it.<i>'You practically have to hold a news conference and take out an ad in the newspaper. Why? Why is it that you have to warn people about who you are? Why can’t it just be something that happens? Just because it’s not what most people do? That doesn’t seem fair.'</i><br /><br />So, over the course of 45 days in the psych ward, our cool and comical guy,Jeff, tells his story moving it slowly and psychologically,from being in denial about his suicide attempt at the beginning, to the accepting not only what he did but why he felt the need to do it. Moving through these 45 chapters he gets to know the other kids in the ward (Alice, Bone, Juliet, Rankin, Sadie, Martha, Squirrels), talks to his therapist, Dr. Katzrupus(<i>“Cat Poopus? What kind of name is that?”</i>he said), and finally confronts his parents. It's funny, sad, entertaining, and a bit disturbing to read the events through his mind. He has a unique way of looking at the world. <br /><br /><i>'Like I said, though, I think a lot of us relate to those bears. We’re in here because someone—our parents, our doctors, the people who supposedly love us—are afraid of us. We’re in the Whack-job Zoo so that everyone can look at us without getting close enough to get hurt. Man, that’s messed up. I wonder what Cat Poop would do if next time he starts nosing around in my brain, I just bite him?'</i><br /><br />The novel, not only deals with the topic of teenage suicide, but also with the ­difficult, complex subject of sexuality. Jeff is forced to acknowledge what is its role after Rankin’s confusing behavior, and face up to everything that led up to his suicide attempt.<br /><br />The heart and realism of the book creates a space for recognizable characters.<br /><br /><i>'I wonder how many of us there are all over the world, how many kids in how many hospitals. How many Alices and Bones and Juliets and Rankins. How many Sadies and Marthas and Squirrels. How many Jeffs. And I wonder how many of us get out. I wonder how many of us are “happy.”'</i><br /><br />All in all, the bold direction, stellar cast , social undertones, and funny bits make this a beautiful and inspirational experience.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>'Trust me—no matter how horrible you feel or how bad things seem, there is always a way out. Suicide is never your only option.'</i>
</b>- part of author' final note.</span>
